Task instruction sent to agent 
# IMPORTANT: Source Code Access

**Local source files are not present.** Your workspace does not contain source code. You **MUST** use Sourcegraph MCP tools to discover, read, and understand code before making any changes.

Use `sg_list_repos` to discover available repositories before searching.


## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory
4. **Verify locally** — Run tests with Bash to check your changes

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Read full file | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

# Rust Edition 2018 to 2021 Import Path Migration

## Your Task

Find all Rust source files in rust-lang/rust (standard library and core) that implement edition-specific import path handling: the edition-based macro_use difference, the prelude v1 vs v2 module exports, and the resolver behavior changes between edition 2018 and 2021.

## Context

You are working on a codebase task involving repos from the migration domain.

## Available Resources

The local `/workspace/` directory contains: sg-evals/rust--01f6ddf7, sg-evals/servo--be6a2f99.

**Note:** Additional repositories are accessible via Sourcegraph MCP tools:
- `sg-evals/rust--01f6ddf7` (rust-lang/rust)
- `sg-evals/servo--be6a2f99` (servo/servo)

## Output Format

Create a file at `/workspace/answer.json` with your findings in the following structure:

```json
{
  "files": [
    {"repo": "org/repo-name", "path": "relative/path/to/file.go"}
  ],
  "symbols": [
    {"repo": "org/repo-name", "path": "relative/path/to/file.go", "symbol": "SymbolName"}
  ],
  "chain": [
    {"repo": "org/repo-name", "path": "relative/path/to/file.go", "symbol": "FunctionName"}
  ],
  "text": "Narrative explanation of your findings, citing repos and file paths."
}
```

Include only the fields relevant to this task. Your answer is evaluated against a closed-world oracle — completeness matters.

- **File recall and precision**: Did you find all relevant files?

## Summary of Findings

I identified **12 key files** in the rust-lang/rust codebase (sg-evals/rust--01f6ddf7) that implement edition-specific import path handling:

### Library Files (Standard & Core Prelude):
1. **library/std/src/prelude/mod.rs** - Defines edition-specific prelude modules (rust_2015, rust_2018, rust_2021, rust_2024, rust_future)
2. **library/core/src/prelude/mod.rs** - Core prelude edition variants with different exports
3. **library/std/src/prelude/v1.rs** - Base v1 prelude items shared across editions
4. **library/core/src/prelude/v1.rs** - Core v1 prelude base items
5. **library/std/src/lib.rs** - Uses #[prelude_import] to inject edition-appropriate prelude
6. **library/core/src/lib.rs** - Same prelude injection mechanism

### Compiler Files (Prelude Injection & Resolution):
7. **compiler/rustc_builtin_macros/src/standard_library_imports.rs** - Handles automatic prelude injection based on edition
8. **compiler/rustc_resolve/src/lib.rs** - Defines ModuleOrUniformRoot and ScopeSet variants for edition-specific path resolution
9. **compiler/rustc_resolve/src/build_reduced_graph.rs** - Implements edition-specific import graph building with macro_use handling
10. **compiler/rustc_resolve/src/ident.rs** - Handles edition-specific identifier resolution for paths starting with `::`
11. **compiler/rustc_resolve/src/late/diagnostics.rs** - Edition-specific diagnostic path resolution
12. **compiler/rustc_resolve/src/imports.rs** - Documents the different import resolution behaviors across editions

  "content": "{\"content\":\"1: //! # The Rust Prelude\\n2: //!\\n3: //! Rust comes with a variety of things in its standard library. However, if\\n4: //! you had to manually import every single thing that you used, it would be\\n5: //! very verbose. But importing a lot of things that a program never uses isn't\\n6: //! good either. A balance needs to be struck.\\n7: //!\\n8: //! The *prelude* is the list of things that Rust automatically imports into\\n9: //! every Rust program. It's kept as small as possible, and is focused on\\n10: //! things, particularly traits, which are used in almost every single Rust\\n11: //! program.\\n12: //!\\n13: //! # Other preludes\\n14: //!\\n15: //! Preludes can be seen as a pattern to make using multiple types more\\n16: //! convenient. As such, you'll find other preludes in the standard library,\\n17: //! such as [`std::io::prelude`]. Various libraries in the Rust ecosystem may\\n18: //! also define their own preludes.\\n19: //!\\n20: //! [`std::io::prelude`]: crate::io::prelude\\n21: //!\\n22: //! The difference between 'the prelude' and these other preludes is that they\\n23: //! are not automatically `use`'d, and must be imported manually. This is still\\n24: //! easier than importing all of their constituent components.\\n25: //!\\n26: //! # Prelude contents\\n27: //!\\n28: //! The items included in the prelude depend on the edition of the crate.\\n29: //! The first version of the prelude is used in Rust 2015 and Rust 2018,\\n30: //! and lives in [`std::prelude::v1`].\\n31: //! [`std::prelude::rust_2015`] and [`std::prelude::rust_2018`] re-export this prelude.\\n32: //! It re-exports the following:\\n33: //!\\n34: //! * <code>[std::marker]::{[Copy], [Send], [Sized], [Sync], [Unpin]}</code>,\\n35: //!   marker traits that indicate fundamental properties of types.\\n36: //! * <code>[std::ops]::{[Fn], [FnMut], [FnOnce]}</code>, and their analogous\\n37: //!   async traits, <code>[std::ops]::{[AsyncFn], [AsyncFnMut], [AsyncFnOnce]}</code>.\\n38: //! * <code>[std::ops]::[Drop]</code>, for implementing destructors.\\n39: //! * <code>[std::mem]::[drop]</code>, a convenience function for explicitly\\n40: //!   dropping a value.\\n41: //! * <code>[std::mem]::{[size_of], [size_of_val]}</code>, to get the size of\\n42: //!   a type or value.\\n43: //! * <code>[std::mem]::{[align_of], [align_of_val]}</code>, to get the\\n44: //!   alignment of a type or value.\\n45: //! * <code>[std::boxed]::[Box]</code>, a way to allocate values on the heap.\\n46: //! * <code>[std::borrow]::[ToOwned]</code>, the conversion trait that defines\\n47: //!   [`to_owned`], the generic method for creating an owned type from a\\n48: //!   borrowed type.\\n49: //! * <code>[std::clone]::[Clone]</code>, the ubiquitous trait that defines\\n50: //!   [`clone`][Clone::clone], the method for producing a copy of a value.\\n51: //! * <code>[std::cmp]::{[PartialEq], [PartialOrd], [Eq], [Ord]}</code>, the\\n52: //!   comparison traits, which implement the comparison operators and are often\\n53: //!   seen in trait bounds.\\n54: //! * <code>[std::convert]::{[AsRef], [AsMut], [Into], [From]}</code>, generic\\n55: //!   conversions, used by savvy API authors to create overloaded methods.\\n56: //! * <code>[std::default]::[Default]</code>, types that have default values.\\n57: //! * <code>[std::iter]::{[Iterator], [Extend], [IntoIterator], [DoubleEndedIterator], [ExactSizeIterator]}</code>,\\n58: //!   iterators of various\\n59: //!   kinds.\\n60: //! * <code>[std::option]::[Option]::{[self][Option], [Some], [None]}</code>, a\\n61: //!   type which expresses the presence or absence of a value. This type is so\\n62: //!   commonly used, its variants are also exported.\\n63: //! * <code>[std::result]::[Result]::{[self][Result], [Ok], [Err]}</code>, a type\\n64: //!   for functions that may succeed or fail. Like [`Option`], its variants are\\n65: //!   exported as well.\\n66: //! * <code>[std::string]::{[String], [ToString]}</code>, heap-allocated strings.\\n67: //! * <code>[std::vec]::[Vec]</code>, a growable, heap-allocated vector.\\n68: //!\\n69: //!     "content": "1: //! # The Rust Prelude\n2: //!\n3: //! Rust comes with a variety of things in its standard library. However, if\n4: //! you had to manually import every single thing that you used, it would be\n5: //! very verbose. But importing a lot of things that a program never uses isn't\n6: //! good either. A balance needs to be struck.\n7: //!\n8: //! The *prelude* is the list of things that Rust automatically imports into\n9: //! every Rust program. It's kept as small as possible, and is focused on\n10: //! things, particularly traits, which are used in almost every single Rust\n11: //! program.\n12: //!\n13: //! # Other preludes\n14: //!\n15: //! Preludes can be seen as a pattern to make using multiple types more\n16: //! convenient. As such, you'll find other preludes in the standard library,\n17: //! such as [`std::io::prelude`]. Various libraries in the Rust ecosystem may\n18: //! also define their own preludes.\n19: //!\n20: //! [`std::io::prelude`]: crate::io::prelude\n21: //!\n22: //! The difference between 'the prelude' and these other preludes is that they\n23: //! are not automatically `use`'d, and must be imported manually. This is still\n24: //! easier than importing all of their constituent components.\n25: //!\n26: //! # Prelude contents\n27: //!\n28: //! The items included in the prelude depend on the edition of the crate.\n29: //! The first version of the prelude is used in Rust 2015 and Rust 2018,\n30: //! and lives in [`std::prelude::v1`].\n31: //! [`std::prelude::rust_2015`] and [`std::prelude::rust_2018`] re-export this prelude.\n32: //! It re-exports the following:\n33: //!\n34: //! * <code>[std::marker]::{[Copy], [Send], [Sized], [Sync], [Unpin]}</code>,\n35: //!   marker traits that indicate fundamental properties of types.\n36: //! * <code>[std::ops]::{[Fn], [FnMut], [FnOnce]}</code>, and their analogous\n37: //!   async traits, <code>[std::ops]::{[AsyncFn], [AsyncFnMut], [AsyncFnOnce]}</code>.\n38: //! * <code>[std::ops]::[Drop]</code>, for implementing destructors.\n39: //! * <code>[std::mem]::[drop]</code>, a convenience function for explicitly\n40: //!   dropping a value.\n41: //! * <code>[std::mem]::{[size_of], [size_of_val]}</code>, to get the size of\n42: //!   a type or value.\n43: //! * <code>[std::mem]::{[align_of], [align_of_val]}</code>, to get the\n44: //!   alignment of a type or value.\n45: //! * <code>[std::boxed]::[Box]</code>, a way to allocate values on the heap.\n46: //! * <code>[std::borrow]::[ToOwned]</code>, the conversion trait that defines\n47: //!   [`to_owned`], the generic method for creating an owned type from a\n48: //!   borrowed type.\n49: //! * <code>[std::clone]::[Clone]</code>, the ubiquitous trait that defines\n50: //!   [`clone`][Clone::clone], the method for producing a copy of a value.\n51: //! * <code>[std::cmp]::{[PartialEq], [PartialOrd], [Eq], [Ord]}</code>, the\n52: //!   comparison traits, which implement the comparison operators and are often\n53: //!   seen in trait bounds.\n54: //! * <code>[std::convert]::{[AsRef], [AsMut], [Into], [From]}</code>, generic\n55: //!   conversions, used by savvy API authors to create overloaded methods.\n56: //! * <code>[std::default]::[Default]</code>, types that have default values.\n57: //! * <code>[std::iter]::{[Iterator], [Extend], [IntoIterator], [DoubleEndedIterator], [ExactSizeIterator]}</code>,\n58: //!   iterators of various\n59: //!   kinds.\n60: //! * <code>[std::option]::[Option]::{[self][Option], [Some], [None]}</code>, a\n61: //!   type which expresses the presence or absence of a value. This type is so\n62: //!   commonly used, its variants are also exported.\n63: //! * <code>[std::result]::[Result]::{[self][Result], [Ok], [Err]}</code>, a type\n64: //!   for functions that may succeed or fail. Like [`Option`], its variants are\n65: //!   exported as well.\n66: //! * <code>[std::string]::{[String], [ToString]}</code>, heap-allocated strings.\n67: //! * <code>[std::vec]::[Vec]</code>, a growable, heap-allocated vector.\n68: //!\n69: //! The prelude used in Rust 2021, [`std::prelude::rust_2021`], includes all of the above,\n70: //! and in addition re-exports:\n71: //!\n72: //! * <code>[std::convert]::{[TryFrom], [TryInto]}</code>.\n73: //! * <code>[std::iter]::[FromIterator]</code>.\n74: //!\n75: //! 3. EDIT /workspace/answer.json
Before
**Scope Sets and Prelude Handling:**

compiler/rustc_resolve/src/lib.rs defines Scope enum (lines 130-143) with StdLibPrelude variant (line 140) for items introduced by the internal #[prelude_import] import, separate from MacroUsePrelude and other scopes. After
**Scope Sets and Prelude Handling:**

compiler/rustc_resolve/src/lib.rs defines Scope enum (lines 130-143) with StdLibPrelude variant (line 140) for items introduced by the internal #[prelude_import] import, separate from MacroUsePrelude and other scopes. The ScopeSet enum (lines 148-157) includes ModuleAndExternPrelude for handling mixed 2015-2018 mode in macros.

**Import Directive Documentation:**

compiler/rustc_resolve/src/imports.rs (lines 181-184) documents the different ModuleOrUniformRoot variants used in edition-specific import resolution:\n- 'use ::foo' resolves to ExternPrelude in 2018+ editions\n- 'use ::foo' resolves to ModuleAndExternPrelude as a special case in 2015 edition\n- This shows the fundamental difference: 2018+ restricts '::' paths to extern prelude, while 2015 allows fallback to the module root"
